<div id="review_809732493" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/809732493" />
    <a title="Cheri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/106692-cheri"><img alt="Cheri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601402708p2/106692.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/809732493?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheri" href="/user/show/106692-cheri">Cheri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=cultural-japan">cultural-japan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=read-in-2014">read-in-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer809732493" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6557843098164723760">Sixteen-year-old Nao has endured the bullying of her schoolmates for years, but when one teacher joins in, the bullying escalates even more. She has no real friends since moving to Tokyo after growing up in Sunnyvale, California, and life in Tokyo bears no resemblance to her happy childhood. Her father is unable to regain the success in employment he had in Sunnyvale, prior to the dot.com bust, along with the associated financial benefits. After his unsuccessful suicide attempt his relationship </span>
  <span id="freeText6557843098164723760" style="display:none">Sixteen-year-old Nao has endured the bullying of her schoolmates for years, but when one teacher joins in, the bullying escalates even more. She has no real friends since moving to Tokyo after growing up in Sunnyvale, California, and life in Tokyo bears no resemblance to her happy childhood. Her father is unable to regain the success in employment he had in Sunnyvale, prior to the dot.com bust, along with the associated financial benefits. After his unsuccessful suicide attempt his relationship with Nao changes, he is withdrawn and feels like a failure at everything. Her mother is overwhelmed with her job and has less time for Nao. The only person that Nao feels connected to, the person she most admires is her great-grandmother, who is a Buddhist nun with a history of somewhat radical political leanings. Documenting her great-grandmother Jiko’s life in a journal becomes her mission, her emotional outlet, her way of dealing with the bullying at school, missing her life in the Silicon Valley and everything that she views as wrong. <br /><br />Ruth, an uninspired author living on an island off the Pacific cost, comes across an odd assortment of items while walking on the beach. Stuffed inside a Hello Kitty lunchbox, Nao tells her story, Old Jiko’s and the story of her great-Uncle. To draw the finder of this treasure in, Nao has included her journal which she specifies, is for this one specific reader, written for her alone. She shares her story in a perky voice, despite all she has been through, and Ruth spends her days thinking of Nao, wondering how she came to find this journal, if Nao is real, if her story is true. <br /><br />I can only say: read it. This is one of my favorite books of the year. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6557843098164723760" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_656093853" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/656093853" />
    <a title="Amanda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1219253-amanda"><img alt="Amanda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1411956191p2/1219253.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/656093853?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amanda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amanda" href="/user/show/1219253-amanda">Amanda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143124870" href="/book/show/18079728-a-tale-for-the-time-being">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1219253-amanda?shelf=blog">blog</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer656093853" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15762394446749568757"><b>3 1/2 Stars</b><br/><br/>You know that experience when you learn something new and only a few days later, references to it start popping up in the most unexpected of places: a television program, a book you're reading, a song on the radio, a friend mentions it in conversation? It's like the universe made certain you knew about this fact or concept because there was fixing to be a pop quiz over it and you needed to be ready. It's these types of connections and coincidences that make up <i>A Tale for the Time Bein</i></span>
  <span id="freeText15762394446749568757" style="display:none"><b>3 1/2 Stars</b><br /><br />You know that experience when you learn something new and only a few days later, references to it start popping up in the most unexpected of places: a television program, a book you're reading, a song on the radio, a friend mentions it in conversation? It's like the universe made certain you knew about this fact or concept because there was fixing to be a pop quiz over it and you needed to be ready. It's these types of connections and coincidences that make up <i>A Tale for the Time Being</i>. While it at first seems as though the novel is filled with sometimes irrelevant facts and digressions, just hang in there--Ruth Ozeki weaves them all together in a tale that serves as a metaphor for how writing and reading, or the interaction between writer and reader, can help us see ourselves in the life of another and ultimately save us from isolation and existential angst.<br /><br /><i>A Tale for the Time Being</i> alternates between two women who, at first, seem very different: Ruth, a writer living on a small Canadian island, and Nao, a teenager living in Japan. When Ruth finds Nao's diary in a Hello Kitty lunchbox, their worlds defy time and space to collide in unexpected ways. The diary, however, is far more serious and sophisticated than its cartoonish packaging might lead one to believe. Written inside of a "hacked" copy of Proust's <i>À la recherche du temps perdu</i> (Nao purchases it in a craft shop in Tokyo that takes old books, guts them, and inserts new paper), Nao directly addresses her unknown reader as one would a close confidant and introduces herself as a time being, one who is aware of and chooses to exist in every moment in time, though it is becoming more and more difficult for her to do so. While reading the diary, Ruth becomes obsessed with finding the girl--at first for fear that Nao may have been a victim of the 2011 tsunami, but later for fear that she is a danger to herself. The diary allows us to experience Nao's unique voice as she relates her inability to fit into her new culture, her father's descent into depression after losing his job in the U.S., and the brutal, horrific bullying she endures at the hands of her classmates and teachers. As her diary goes on, the faux title begins to prove true: Nao is becoming more lost as time goes on. <br /><br />As Ruth reads the diary and desperately searches for Nao, we learn about her life as well and find that the two women overlap in surprising ways: both are Japanese-Americans, both are transplants to a place and culture not their own, both have somewhat strained relationships with the men in their lives, both have strong connections to a revered female elder, both feel a failed sense to accomplish what they want in their writing, both have an expatriate's experience of 9/11, both worry about losing time. Nao's name often functions as a pun on the word "Now," leading to overlapping meanings as to what both Ruth and Nao, feeling stuck in time, may really be searching for--hope for a "now" in which they can fully exist without being immobilized by fear, worry, or sorrow. <br /><br />While I enjoyed the book, I can't say that I loved it. Ozeki's meditations on time and existence are beautifully rendered, but sometimes difficult to understand as they rely upon the reader to retain information from previous chapters when they are echoed in later events. There is so much here and so much that I don't fully comprehend. For not only is this a story about relationships, but it's also one about the concept of time, especially as it relates to zen and quantum physics. Ozeki plays with the idea of parallel universes, of time and existence as nonlinear. While I was able to keep up with the general idea, I still feel like there's a whole layer of meaning that I kept grasping for without success. This is a book that I think I could really love upon a second or third reading as I think more and more tumblers would fall into place and allow me to really unlock the full depth of meaning here.<br /><br />Surprisingly, though, I had the opposite reaction to many readers in that I often found the Ruth chapters more compelling than the Nao chapters. Nao's diary doesn't really read like a diary; instead it reads like a first person narrative. And, yeah, okay, a diary is a first person narrative, but it usually doesn't read like a novel as it's more bare bones in terms of physical details, focusing more on the emotional inner life of its writer. Nao's voice also reads more like that of an adult; for a teenager, she is very precocious and while the details of her life as an adolescent are rendered authentically, she herself doesn't sound much like a teen. In Ruth, Ozeki excels at capturing the subtle seismic shifts in a marriage and, if one pays close attention, there's much about Ruth that makes her the perfect recipient for Nao's diary. I also enjoyed the tension created by whether or not Ruth will be successful in her search for Nao--especially since the Nao she is looking for is one of the past and may or may not exist in the present, regardless of whether or not she can be physically found.<br /><br />Cross posted at <a href="http://viiamanda.blogspot.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">This Insignificant Cinder</a> and at <a href="http://www.shelfinflicted.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Shelf Inflicted</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="15762394446749568757" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('sxbYRTNlsj7NuT/7Ov7dtuCMInvOGqHsELqzRuZuCeWJMLGXiDt7VmqSi5rNC9dpNtPFswCXL4tLxPX3Ky37fw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fcqK+eTI16/Doyweyl7+PRiBYAYxJ7N3dNKB3SlsTc1H7OMrX5Yex2SImH89q/Tizt6Hzv+qPRAvrMds5C+/Vw==')})
  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8395535921904825101">Ruth Ozeki (born in New Haven, Connecticut) is a Japanese American novelist. She is the daughter of anthropologist Floyd Lounsbury.<br/><br/>Ozeki published her debut novel, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12349.My_Year_of_Meats' title='My Year of Meats' rel='nofollow noopener'>My Year of Meats</a>, in 1998. She followed up with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/917346.All_Over_Creation' title='All Over Creation' rel='nofollow noopener'>All Over Creation</a> in 2003. Her new novel, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15811545.A_Tale_for_the_Time_Being' title='A Tale for the Time Being' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Tale for the Time Being</a>, was published on March 12, 2013. <br/><br/>She is married to Canadian land artist <a href='http://www.oliverk.org' rel='nofollow noopener'>Oliver Kellhammer</a>, and the couple divides their</span>
  <span id="freeText8395535921904825101" style="display:none">Ruth Ozeki (born in New Haven, Connecticut) is a Japanese American novelist. She is the daughter of anthropologist Floyd Lounsbury.<br /><br />Ozeki published her debut novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12349.My_Year_of_Meats" title="My Year of Meats" rel="nofollow noopener">My Year of Meats</a>, in 1998. She followed up with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/917346.All_Over_Creation" title="All Over Creation" rel="nofollow noopener">All Over Creation</a> in 2003. Her new novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15811545.A_Tale_for_the_Time_Being" title="A Tale for the Time Being" rel="nofollow noopener">A Tale for the Time Being</a>, was published on March 12, 2013. <br /><br />She is married to Canadian land artist <a href="http://www.oliverk.org" rel="nofollow noopener">Oliver Kellhammer</a>, and the couple divides their time between New York City and Vancouver.</span>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/21536809-a-tale-for-the-time-being">Quotes from A Tale for the Ti...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Sometimes when she told stories about the past her eyes would get teary from all the memories she had, but they weren't tears. She wasn't crying. They were just the memories, leaking out.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
